# Excel: PivotTables in Depth

> PivotTables are one of the most powerful features in Excel, yet many users are barely scratching the surface of what they can do. In this course, Excel power user Curt Frye shows you how to navigate the complexity of PivotTables while taking advantage of their power. Curt explains how to build PivotTables from single or multiple data sources, pivot and configure a PivotTable, define a default Pivo

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270) | 3h 53m | Intermediate | 247K learners
> [Jump to Path Context ↓](#path-context)

### 2. 1. Creating and Pivoting PivotTables

#### Introduce PivotTables
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Before I go into the details of how to create and manipulate pivot tables, I'd like to demonstrate a few of the ways that pivot tables help you analyze your worksheet data.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=10)** I have opened my sample file, 01_01_introduction, and I'm on sheet two, which has a pivot table.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=17)** And this pivot table might look a lot like a cross tab that you would create in your own worksheets by hand.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=24)** So I have column labels for my two different types of customers, business and individual, and then a range of sales associates based on their job title.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=34)** However, a pivot table is a lot more than just a static representation of your data.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=40)** Over on the right you can see the pivot table fields task pane, and the task pane contains the tools that you can use to manipulate and change the appearance of your pivot table.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=51)** At the top is the field list, and these are all the fields that are available for use within the pivot table.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=57)** Any one that has a green check mark next to it, such as employee title, customer type, and then all the way at the bottom, order total, means that they currently appear within the pivot table.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=70)** The fields area below that has a number of areas currently in use.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=75)** Columns, which has my column headers for customer type, rows for the row headers, that's employee title, and then the values are currently displaying sum of order total, and that reflects the organization within the pivot table I described earlier.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=93)** If I want to change the organization of the pivot table, I can drag fields from place to place.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=99)** So in the pivot table fields task pane, I'll drag customer type below rows, employee title, and I have business and individual sales for each of my employee titles or employee levels.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=115)** If I want to change the order or organization of the fields and have customer type on top, then I can drag employee title below customer type within the rows area, and I have customer type as the top level of organization, and then the employee titles below that.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=134)** If I want to filter my data, for example, you can narrow it down to just senior sales associate and senior sales representative, then I can do that by adding a filter.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=146)** So go up to the row labels control, click its down arrow, and then employee title is already selected.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=153)** I could also select customer type, and then I will clear the select all checkbox and then click senior sales associate and senior sales representative and okay, and my filter has been applied.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=170)** One final thing that I'll show you is how to change the appearance of your data by applying a style.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=177)** I'll click a cell within the body of the pivot table and you can see the style has already been applied.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=183)** If you want to change it, then go to the design contextual tab of the ribbon, which appears when you have a cell within a pivot table selected, and then go to the pivot styles gallery and select a style.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=197)** I will go with Light blue, pivot style medium six, just as an example, and you can see how its appearance has changed.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=206)** I hope this brief demonstration has given you a feel for pivot table's capabilities.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/introduce-pivottables?u=76281980&t=210)** In the remaining movies in this course, I'll go into much more detail on how to create and manipulate pivot tables and the data they contain.

#### Format data for use in a PivotTable
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Pivot tables let you summarize and reorganize your data dynamically, but you can't use just any data set.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=7)** In most cases, your data needs to be laid out as a data list and preferably as an Excel table.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=15)** In this movie, I will describe how your data needs to be laid out for creating a pivot table.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=21)** My sample file is 01_02_DataLayout, and you can find it in the chapter one folder of the exercise files collection.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=30)** In this workbook, I have a subset of the data that we'll use throughout the course.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=36)** So I have columns of data for order number, order date, order type, and the others you see there.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=43)** And then each row represents a particular set of facts about an order.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=49)** So we have the order number for the first order in the list there, then the date, then the order type, customer type, product category, and the total.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=59)** There are a couple of things I'd like to point out about this data.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=62)** The first is that we have a blank column and a blank row to the right and below the data that's going to be used.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=73)** That means that we have a specific area that contains the data that we want, and there won't be any extra data added that we don't want.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=83)** Also, there are no empty rows or no blank rows within the body of the data.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=89)** So if I were to delete the values from cell B10 through F10, we could still create a pivot table using this data.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=98)** The only difference is that for order number 1103804, we wouldn't know anything about it except the number.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=107)** So the order date, order type, and all the other values have been deleted and would show up as blanks within the dataset.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=114)** If I were to delete the value in A10, then we no longer have a valid data list, and the reason is that Excel would look at row one through nine as one dataset and 11 down through 21 as the second.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=133)** So I'll press Ctrl + Z to bring those values back.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=139)** Finally, it is helpful, though not strictly necessary to change the formatting of your column headers so that they are different than the data below.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=149)** That will make it easier for Excel to identify them as column headers and use them as labels when you create your pivot table.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=156)** This data is currently laid out as a data list and it doesn't have any extra structure to it.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=163)** You can create a pivot table using this dataset, but if I were to add a row of data below it, then it wouldn't be included automatically in the pivot table.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=174)** However, what I can do is create an Excel table, and that would include updates whenever you refresh the pivot table after the data is added.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=184)** So I will click any cell within the data list, and then on the home tab of the ribbon, click format as table.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=192)** And then I'll select a format.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=194)** In this case, I'll just see the first one, Light.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=199)** Where's the data for your table?
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=201)** And I can see from the marquee outline that Excel has correctly identified the data and also my table has headers is checked.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=212)** If for some reason Excel hadn't been able to detect that you had headers, in this case, by different formatting, then that checkbox would be blank.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=222)** So if you do have headers and they're just the same format as the rest of the values, you could check this box and Excel would include the first row as column headers.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=233)** This case, I'll click OK, and I get my Excel table.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=239)** It looks the same, but you also notice that there is the table design contextual tab on the ribbon, and that allows you to do a number of things to your table.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=249)** One very useful thing is to change the table name.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=253)** So I will click in the table name box in the properties group, and I'll type sales data.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=261)** So that is now the name of the Excel table that I created.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=265)** And another thing I'll demonstrate is to add a total row.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=269)** We currently have order total in column F, but we don't know the total or other summary.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=276)** So on the table design contextual tab, I will check total row, and at the bottom I get the total of all sales for the data that I have here.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=287)** Once you have your source data arranged in the data list, you can create a pivot table.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-data-for-use-in-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=293)** However, if possible, I do recommend creating an Excel table because of the extra abilities that it gives you.

#### Pivot a PivotTable

>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=6)** A task that would take you several minutes if done by hand, takes just a few seconds when you summarize your data using a pivot table.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=13)** So in this movie, I will show you how to pivot a pivot table.

>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=34)** If you don't see it, then it is probably hidden.

>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=43)** If it's not selected, it goes away, even if you have a cell clicked within the pivot table.

>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=59)** So if I go down to the fields area, then I see columns says customer type, rows says product category and value says sum of order total.

>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=76)** You can see it reflected here, business first.

>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=128)** So I'll drag it from the rows area, in this case up to the field list.

>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=169)** One final thing I'll demonstrate is how to defer a layout update.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=173)** If you're working with data on your computer itself, then you probably don't need to do this.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=178)** However, if you are working with data over a network and you have a slow connection or slower connection, or if you're working with millions of rows, then you want to defer the layout update.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=193)** And at the bottom of the pivot table fields task pane, there is the defer layout update button.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=201)** So I will check that and I will drag sales region off of the pivot table and you can see that the pivot table did not update.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=212)** I'll drag employee title down to rows and then I'll drag product category off and I'll drag down order type.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=222)** Those are all the changes I want to make so I'll click update and the pivot table organization and summary has been updated.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=230)** But note that defer layout update is still checked.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=234)** If I want to have my updates occur as I make them, then I will clear that checkbox, everything looks good, and I'll drag order type below employee title, and I get the change that you would expect to the pivot tables organization.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=252)** Changing a pivot tables arrangement shifts the data's emphasis, enabling you to examine the data from different perspectives quickly and easily.

#### Configure a PivotTable
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/configure-a-pivottable?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/configure-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=5)** You can show, hide or, in some cases, move those elements to change the PivotTable configuration to your liking.

>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/configure-a-pivottable?u=76281980&t=108)** And you could do this if you have some very long field names.

### 3. 2. Summarizing PivotTable Data

#### Manage subtotals and grand totals

>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=6)** Pivoting and filtering the PivotTable changes how the individual data elements are displayed, but you can also position the subtotals and grand totals for rows and columns to summarize your data as desired.

> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=19)** In this movie, I will show you how to work with subtotals and grand totals in a PivotTable.

> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=66)** If I drag customer type to the columns area, so I'll go over to the PivotTable Fields task pane, and if you don't see that, make sure that you have a cell in the PivotTable selected.

> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=80)** Click PivotTable Analyze and then make sure Field List is selected.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=85)** I'll drag customer-typed columns, and two things happened.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=90)** The first is that the subtotals went away because I only have one field in the rows area, so there's nothing to subtotal.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=99)** Instead, I have a grand total for the columns.

> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=101)** And now I also have a new grand total for the rows.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=105)** Now I will undo my last change by pressing Control + Z.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=110)** And let's say that I want to move the subtotals to the bottom of the group.

> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=115)** So instead of seeing the total for business next to the label, I will see it at the bottom, below the bottom of the group.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=123)** To do that, make sure you have any cell in the PivotTable selected.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=127)** Then go up to the Design contextual tab, and in the Layout group at the far left, click the Subtotals button and you have three options.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=138)** Don't show subtotals, show all subtotals at bottom or show them at top.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=145)** Show subtotals at the top of the group is what Excel does by default.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=151)** However, I prefer to have subtotals at the bottom, so I will click Show all Subtotals at Bottom of Group.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=158)** And you can see the PivotTable organization has changed slightly.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=162)** I have a blank cell to the right of Business, and then I have Business Total at the bottom.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=167)** And to me, that makes more sense because I read the values from top to bottom and I would expect the total to appear at the bottom.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=177)** And of course, that is completely my preference.

> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=180)** If you like to have the subtotals at the top, then by all means, either leave them there or move them there.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=188)** To show you what the PivotTable looks like when subtotals are turned off, I'll go back to the Subtotals control.

>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=200)** And I'll press Control + Z to undo that change.

> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=204)** Grand totals work very much the same way, except that you can only turn them on or off.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=209)** You can't move them.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=210)** A grand total for a column will appear at the bottom and grand totals for rows will appear at the right.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=217)** So I'll move customer type back to the columns area, and I have a Grand Total column, which summarizes each of the rows.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=227)** And then Grand Total row, which summarizes each of the columns.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=232)** To work with Grand Totals, you go up to the Layout group again on the Design contextual tab of the Ribbon.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=238)** Click Grand Totals, and you have four options.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=241)** You can turn them off for rows and columns, on for rows and columns, on for rows only, and on for columns only.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=250)** If I were to click On for Columns Only, then I don't get my product category grand totals anymore.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=257)** If I show them for rows only, then two things happen.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=264)** First is that the grand total for the columns is hidden or removed, and also, because the cell that was selected is now outside of the PivotTable, then the PivotTable task pane and the contextual tabs on the Ribbon have gone away.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=281)** So to bring them back, just click any cell within the PivotTable.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=287)** And then Grand Totals, I'll just do off for both.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=290)** So that's what that looks like.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=292)** I actually prefer to have them on for both rows and columns if I have that much data and that type of organization.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=299)** So there we go.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=300)** Subtotals and grand totals provide easy-to-read summaries of the values in your PivotTables' rows and columns.
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/manage-subtotals-and-grand-totals?u=76281980&t=307)** If you find those extra rows and columns are a distraction, or if you just want to emphasize the values in the body of the data area, then you can hide them quite easily.

#### Summarize more than one data field

> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/summarize-more-than-one-data-field?u=76281980&t=7)** And while that's certainly the most common layout, you can summarize more than one value field at a time.

>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/summarize-more-than-one-data-field?u=76281980&t=111)** So it still hasn't taken the two away because it needs to distinguish the name of the field from the other copy.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/summarize-more-than-one-data-field?u=76281980&t=121)** Because of the way the labels are constructed, pivot tables with multiple data sets, especially if you have multiple values in the columns area, can be very wide on your screen.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/summarize-more-than-one-data-field?u=76281980&t=134)** So while you can create a dashboard-usable pivot tables that have multiple data fields, in fact, you have to remove the labels and it makes them hard to read.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/summarize-more-than-one-data-field?u=76281980&t=145)** So in most cases, it's best to go with one.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/summarize-more-than-one-data-field?u=76281980&t=148)** But if you have a lot of screen area to use, then you can use it like you see here.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/summarize-more-than-one-data-field?u=76281980&t=154)** And of course, you can always hide the pivot table field's task pane if some of your data is hidden.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/summarize-more-than-one-data-field?u=76281980&t=160)** So I'll click the Close button, and the data is now displayed in your pivot table worksheet.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/summarize-more-than-one-data-field?u=76281980&t=169)** Pivot tables can get a bit crowded when you display more than one data field in the values area, but if you use the space wisely, you can gain a lot of insights by viewing two or more data sets simultaneously.

#### Drill down to the underlying data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/drill-down-to-the-underlying-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/drill-down-to-the-underlying-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Pivot tables enable you to find a specific data points within your set.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/drill-down-to-the-underlying-data?u=76281980&t=6)** For example, if you wanted to know the total value of sales of electric vehicle chargers made by sales associates, then you could create this pivot table and look at cell C5 where you have the EV charger column intersecting with the Sales Associate row.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/drill-down-to-the-underlying-data?u=76281980&t=27)** What it doesn't tell you is what the data is that went into creating that total.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/drill-down-to-the-underlying-data?u=76281980&t=34)** However, you can find that, in most cases, by drilling down within a pivot table.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/drill-down-to-the-underlying-data?u=76281980&t=40)** To drill down and see the rows of data that contribute to a particular cell, double-click the cell.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/drill-down-to-the-underlying-data?u=76281980&t=47)** So in this case, I will double-click cell C5, and Excel creates a new worksheet called Detail1 that shows the three rows with data that contributed to the total.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/drill-down-to-the-underlying-data?u=76281980&t=63)** There are a couple of things that I'll point out.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/drill-down-to-the-underlying-data?u=76281980&t=65)** The first is that all of the fields from the original data source are included, not just those that are in the pivot table.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/drill-down-to-the-underlying-data?u=76281980&t=73)** And secondly, you get a nice summary at the top, which indicates what the details are summarized for within this worksheet.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/drill-down-to-the-underlying-data?u=76281980&t=83)** The other thing I'll point out is that sometimes values with large numbers are displayed in scientific notation.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/drill-down-to-the-underlying-data?u=76281980&t=91)** So if I click cell A4, you can see that I have a value here, and it is displayed as nine times E to the 08 or the +08, and that means that it is nine times 10 to the eighth and with a little bit left over, so you're not seeing the full employee ID.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/drill-down-to-the-underlying-data?u=76281980&t=113)** You are actually just seeing a summary of it in scientific notation.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/drill-down-to-the-underlying-data?u=76281980&t=119)** If you want to change values like that from scientific notation to a full value, then you can click the table column header to select the entire column.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/drill-down-to-the-underlying-data?u=76281980&t=132)** Then on the Home tab, go up, and now I'll click Number, but I have decimal places that I don't want, so I will, again, on the Home tab, the number group click decrease decimal twice, and I just have my whole numbers.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/drill-down-to-the-underlying-data?u=76281980&t=150)** It is possible to drill down into a data source even if the pivot table draws this data from an external table.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/drill-down-to-the-underlying-data?u=76281980&t=157)** You can do that because Excel creates something internally called the pivot cache, and it uses that instead of the source table.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/drill-down-to-the-underlying-data?u=76281980&t=165)** If you're done using the worksheet, you can right click its sheet tab, click Delete, Confirm, and it will go away, and you continue working with the data in the pivot table summary.

### 4. 3. Sorting and Filtering PivotTable Data

#### Sort PivotTable data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/sort-pivottable-data?u=76281980)

>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/create-a-custom-sort-order?u=76281980&t=306)** So if you see something that's out of place, go back in using the Excel Options dialogue box to edit the list and ensure that you have no blank lines and also that you have no extra spaces or misspellings that could cause the sort criteria to fail.

> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-field-by-selection?u=76281980&t=184)** If you have more than one field in either the rows or the columns area, then you need to do something different to use the labels within the pivot table to get to the filter controls.

> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-field-by-selection?u=76281980&t=197)** So I will drag Product Category below Sales Region in the Rows area.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-field-by-selection?u=76281980&t=203)** Now, I don't have a columns label, so I need to use row labels to change the filters of both fields.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-field-by-selection?u=76281980&t=210)** So I'll click the row labels down arrow.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-field-by-selection?u=76281980&t=214)** And at the top, I have a new control called Select Field.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-field-by-selection?u=76281980&t=217)** So I have Sales Region, and if I click the down arrow next to it, then I get Product Category.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-field-by-selection?u=76281980&t=225)** So now I can work with Product Category, and I will add back all of the items.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-field-by-selection?u=76281980&t=231)** And I did that by selecting all, so that's good.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-field-by-selection?u=76281980&t=236)** I'll click OK.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-field-by-selection?u=76281980&t=237)** And all of the values are back.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-field-by-selection?u=76281980&t=240)** Filtering by selection gives you pinpoint control over the values that appear in your pivot table.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-field-by-selection?u=76281980&t=245)** You should use this type of filter when you want to show or hide a few values from the display.

#### Filter a PivotTable using slicers
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-using-slicers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-using-slicers?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you filter a pivot table, Excel indicates that a field has been filtered by placing different icons in the body of the pivot table and in the pivot table field list.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-using-slicers?u=76281980&t=11)** Unfortunately, those icons are small and hard to see and what's more, they give you no indication of which values are included or excluded from the filter.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-using-slicers?u=76281980&t=21)** However, you can visually indicate which items are included or excluded from filter by using slicers, and in this movie, I will show you how to use them.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-using-slicers?u=76281980&t=32)** My sample file is 03_06_Slicer, and you can find it in the chapter three folder of the exercise files collection.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-using-slicers?u=76281980&t=41)** In this pivot table and you can see the fields that are available in the pivot table fields list, I have my product categories and then my customer types.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-using-slicers?u=76281980&t=54)** If I want to filter them and have visual indicators of what's included and excluded, then I can click any cell within the pivot table and then go up to the pivot table analyze contextual tab, and click insert slicer.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-using-slicers?u=76281980&t=72)** The insert slicers dialogue box appears and from here, I can select the field or fields for which I want to create a slicer.
>

> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=9)** But suppose you have a terrific pivot table layout and want to filter it using the values in a field that you don't want to appear in the body of the pivot table?

> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=101)** And at the top, I have sales region in the filters area.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=105)** And if I click the filter header down arrow, I get a list of all the items that are present within the sales region field.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=119)** If I want to display just a single region, then I can click, in this case, Central East and click okay.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=128)** And I only see sales that are associated with that region.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=131)** And you can see that I have Central East in my filter label.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=137)** I'll click the filter arrow again, and this time, I'll check the select multiple items box.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=144)** And that gives me check boxes.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=147)** So I will add Northeast and southeast and click okay.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=154)** And now, I have multiple items displayed, but instead of having the items broken down as, for example, a row header or a column header, in this case, the filter area label says multiple items.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=169)** And if I click the filter arrow, I can see what they are.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=174)** If I want to bring everything back, I can click all.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=178)** Click okay, and there they are.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=181)** You might be curious what happens if you drag a copy of a field that's currently in the filter area to either the rows or columns area, and let's see what happens there.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=193)** I'll drag sales region from the field list down to the rows area, and it's been removed from the filters area and added to the body of the pivot table.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=205)** So you can't have it in both places at once.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=208)** I'll just drag it back up.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=211)** One final thing to show is that if you have a field in the report filters area, then you can create separate pivot tables on separate worksheets for each of the items in that field.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=227)** So with sales region in the filters area, I'll go up to pivot table analyze, then I'll click options, and I'll click show report filter pages, show all report filter pages of sales region.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=243)** That's the only field I have, so it comes selected, click okay, and Excel creates a separate worksheet for each of the regions.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=253)** What it is is a new pivot table that has a filter applied.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=258)** So I have Central East and then Control + Page Down to move to North Central East.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=264)** Control + Page Down, North Central West, Northeast, and the other worksheets through there for each of the regions In my dataset.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=275)** You can have multiple fields in the filters area, but if you create report filter pages based on those two fields, then you can get what's called a combinatorial explosion.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=286)** So if you have nine sales regions and nine say, employee categories, then you would have nine times nine or 81 separate worksheets created within your pivot table.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/filter-a-pivottable-with-report-filter-fields?u=76281980&t=301)** So I don't recommend doing it for values in more than one field, but it's your workbook and your data, so do as you see fit.

### 5. 4. Formatting PivotTables

#### Apply a PivotTable style
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you create a PivotTable, Excel applies some formatting that lets you distinguish the labels and organizational layers from the data in the body of the PivotTable.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=11)** Excel comes with a substantial number of built-in styles from which to choose.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=15)** So if you want to change your PivotTable's formatting, you can do so quickly.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=19)** In this movie, I will show you how to work with formatting in a PivotTable and apply styles.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=27)** My sample file is 04_01_ApplyStyle, and you can find it in the chapter four folder of the exercise files collection.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=35)** This workbook contains a PivotTable and it has some basic formatting applied to it.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=42)** I have a background color for my headers and also for the grand total and the values in the grand total and also the labels.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=55)** For the row labels control column labels control, and retail and wholesale sales types are all bold as well.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=64)** So that's the overall formatting.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=66)** But before I show you how to work with styles, I want to show you what happens when you apply formatting to an individual cell.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=73)** If I were to click cell B8 and add yellow fill color to that cell, then that specific cell is now formatted with the yellow background color.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=86)** If I pivot the PivotTable, that is change its organization by dragging OrderType from the columns area to the rows area below sales region, then the same cell is still visible with the yellow background because the organizational layers of the PivotTable haven't changed.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=105)** They've just moved into a new position.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=108)** And I will drag OrderType back up and scroll up.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=114)** If, however, I were to remove OrderType from the columns area and drag it off the PivotTable entirely, then the organizational levels have changed and the same cell does not appear within the body of the PivotTable.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=131)** In other words, I no longer have retail and wholesale broken out separately.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=136)** I just have the sum.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=138)** Now, if I press Control + Z to undo, it comes back.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=142)** However, if I press Control + Y, of course, it disappears because the organization changed.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=148)** If I drag OrderType back down to the columns area, then the formatting is gone because the PivotTable essentially discards it as soon as the organization changes.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=162)** So that's how formatting works with individual cells within the PivotTable.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=166)** If you want to affect the appearance of the PivotTable overall, you can apply a style.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=172)** To do that, click any cell in the body of the PivotTable, go to the design tab, and then in the PivotTable styles group, you'll see the PivotTable styles gallery.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=183)** And here you can apply different styles.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=185)** For example, I could go for instead of the blue, a light gray, and if there are options available, for example, banded rows or columns, you can go to the PivotTable style options group and check the box and they're applied.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=201)** Also note that the previews change.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=204)** You can scroll through the PivotTable styles gallery one row at a time by clicking the up and down arrow buttons, or you can click the more button and see all of the styles that are available to you.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=220)** You can apply a PivotTable style from the gallery by clicking the style as I did before, but you have a couple of options if you right click.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=228)** So I will right click one of the styles, light blue, pivot style light nine, and right click and gives you a shortcut menu.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=236)** You can apply in clear formatting, which removes any formatting that had already been applied.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=242)** Apply and maintain formatting, which will, as the name implies, keep any formatting that you added manually.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=250)** You can also duplicate if you're creating a new style, set it as a default or add the gallery to the quick access toolbar.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=258)** If you just click a style, then Excel will apply and maintain formatting.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=263)** However, if you right click and click apply and clear formatting, then Excel will do just that.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=270)** It will remove any formatting you've applied and apply the style.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=274)** I'll go ahead and click that and we get our formatting applied.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=280)** Also note that even though I have banded rows checked, banded rows are not available with a style, so they're not displayed.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=288)** PivotTable styles are a part of Office themes, which are collections of predefined color schemes.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=294)** So if you apply a built-in PivotTable style and then change the Office theme, the built-in PivotTable style changes as well.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=302)** To see the Office themes that are available, go to page layout.

>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=314)** I'll move my mouse pointer over gallery and you can see in the background how the PivotTable formatting has changed.

> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=324)** You can edit themes, for example, by changing color, font or effects that are applied, and you can go to themes and browse elsewhere for current themes.

> **[5:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=343)** So remember, regardless of the style you apply, you can always change the formatting of individual cells.

> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/apply-a-pivottable-style?u=76281980&t=349)** The formatting just might not stick around if you change the organizational levels of your PivotTable.

>

> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/change-the-pivottable-layout?u=76281980&t=186)** Finally, you can add a blank row between individual items.

> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/change-the-pivottable-layout?u=76281980&t=190)** So again, on the design contextual tab, I'll click blank rows and click insert blank line after each item and Excel does just that.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/change-the-pivottable-layout?u=76281980&t=201)** In this case, row nine is a blank line after the sales associate total and sales associates one's first value.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/change-the-pivottable-layout?u=76281980&t=210)** If I were to change the report layout by clicking and then going back to compact form, then I have a layout which is a compromise between compact form and adding lines so that you have room to see where your data is.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/change-the-pivottable-layout?u=76281980&t=226)** So here I have the subtotals at the top of each group, but there's a blank line, so it's easier to see that the value, currently cell B10, is associated with sales associate one.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/change-the-pivottable-layout?u=76281980&t=239)** Modifying pivot table layouts lets you present your data as effectively as possible, both for your personal viewing and as part of a presentation.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/change-the-pivottable-layout?u=76281980&t=247)** You should take the time to experiment with these options so you can decide which will work best for your own use.

#### Format cells using color scales
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-cells-using-color-scales?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-cells-using-color-scales?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One useful way to summarize data visually is by using a color scale, which uses a cell's value to determine which fill color to assign to the cell.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-cells-using-color-scales?u=76281980&t=11)** Unlike rule-based formats that either apply a color or not, the color scale conditional format applies a color from a two or three color gradient.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-cells-using-color-scales?u=76281980&t=21)** And in this movie, I will show you how to create and apply them.

> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-cells-using-color-scales?u=76281980&t=26)** My sample file is 05_04_ColorScale, and you can find it in the chapter five folder of the Exercise files collection.

>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/format-cells-using-color-scales?u=76281980&t=214)** So for that reason and others, I tend to stay with the two color color scales.

#### Format cells using icon sets

> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/control-how-multiple-rules-are-applied?u=76281980&t=143)** And you can see in the background that the bold type went away.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/control-how-multiple-rules-are-applied?u=76281980&t=149)** So the only rule that is being applied is the one with the green background and regular text.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/control-how-multiple-rules-are-applied?u=76281980&t=155)** And as we can see from looking at the data, that will always be the case because all of the top three values are over 350,000.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/control-how-multiple-rules-are-applied?u=76281980&t=163)** So when Excel saw that the top rule was true, it stopped looking to see if any other additional rules were true as well.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/control-how-multiple-rules-are-applied?u=76281980&t=171)** And that means that this rule will never be applied in this case.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/control-how-multiple-rules-are-applied?u=76281980&t=177)** So I'll clear stop if true for the first rule.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/control-how-multiple-rules-are-applied?u=76281980&t=182)** Now let's see what happens if I change the order in which the rules are applied.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/control-how-multiple-rules-are-applied?u=76281980&t=186)** So I will make sure that the top rule is clicked and then click the move down button so that the rule order is changed and I'll click apply.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/control-how-multiple-rules-are-applied?u=76281980&t=199)** Now what's happening is that the top three rules have bold text and a regular background, and any other value over 350,000 has a green background.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/control-how-multiple-rules-are-applied?u=76281980&t=214)** And if we look over at the data, we can see that that is in fact how the rules have been applied.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/control-how-multiple-rules-are-applied?u=76281980&t=219)** If I were to check the stop if true box next to the rule that's now on top, the one for the top three values and click apply, then nothing changes because the behavior of this particular rule set operates the same way whether we stop or we don't stop.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/control-how-multiple-rules-are-applied?u=76281980&t=240)** When you're done working with your conditional formatting, you can click OK and close the dialog box.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/control-how-multiple-rules-are-applied?u=76281980&t=247)** It can take some time to get used to how Excel applies multiple conditional formatting rules, create the rules that you want, make sure they work on their own, and then experiment with different orders, and using stop if true, to apply them exactly the way you want.

>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/create-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=189)** Pivot charts look like regular Excel charts, and in many ways they are, but there are some important differences.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/create-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=196)** First, you can't switch the row and column orientation of a pivot chart using the select data source dialogue box.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/create-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=203)** However, of course, you can rearrange your data by pivoting so you can get the same functionality just in a different way.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/create-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=211)** Also, there are some chart types that you cannot create, and those include the XY scatter chart, stock charts or bubble charts.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/create-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=219)** And finally, pivoting a pivot chart removes any trend lines, data labels, error bars, or anything else that you have applied to the chart.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/create-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=227)** So if you have done a lot of work adding elements to a pivot chart, it might be a good idea to create another copy of it, for example, based on another pivot table on a separate worksheet so you don't lose the one you're working with.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/create-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=241)** Or you can just make a backup copy of your workbook and make the changes in the new copy.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/create-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=247)** Finally, one thing I'll show here is that if you want to have your pivot chart on a separate worksheet from the pivot table, then make sure that you click the body of the pivot chart.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/create-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=259)** And then on the pivot chart, analyze contextual tab on the ribbon.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/create-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=263)** Go to the actions group and click move chart.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/create-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=267)** Choose where you want the chart to be placed.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/create-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=269)** In this case, I'll put it on a new sheet called Chart 1.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/create-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=273)** Click okay.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/create-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=275)** And there it is.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/create-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=276)** If we go back to sheet one, which had the pivot table and the pivot chart, you can see that we only have the pivot table and the chart is on chart one.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/create-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=288)** Pivot charts let you summarize your data visually, providing an overview and opening the door to insights, you might not discover from looking at the numbers themselves.

#### Pivot a PivotChart
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivotchart?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The real power of a pivot chart comes when you rearrange your data dynamically.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=4)** A task that would take several minutes if you did it by hand will take just a few seconds when you summarize your data using a pivot chart.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=12)** And in this movie, I'll go into some detail on how to pivot data within a pivot chart.

> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=20)** My sample file is 06_02_Pivot, and you can find it in the Chapter 6 folder of the exercise files collection.

> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=28)** In this workbook on sheet one, I have a pivot table and a blank pivot chart.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=33)** So I'll start by adding fields from the PivotChart Fields task pane on the right.

> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=39)** So I will drag Sales Region down to the Categories area, and then I will scroll down on the Field list and drag Order Total to Values.

> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=53)** And here I have my order total.
>

> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=67)** If I want to show more detail, then I can add fields to the pivot chart.

> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/pivot-a-pivotchart?u=76281980&t=71)** To add a field, I will scroll up in the Fields list, and in this case, I'll drag down Customer Type, and I'll add that to the Legend or Series area.

> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/change-a-pivotchart-s-layout?u=76281980&t=166)** So if I go up again to the design contextual tab, chart layouts group and click Add Chart Element, then I can select what and often where I want to add.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/change-a-pivotchart-s-layout?u=76281980&t=178)** So I'll point to Chart Title, and I can do above chart or a centered overlay.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/change-a-pivotchart-s-layout?u=76281980&t=183)** In this case, I'll go with above chart so it doesn't obscure any of the data.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/change-a-pivotchart-s-layout?u=76281980&t=188)** There it is, and I won't bother editing the text.

> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/change-a-pivotchart-s-layout?u=76281980&t=191)** You can use the technique that I showed you before.

> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/change-a-pivotchart-s-layout?u=76281980&t=195)** I might also want to add axis titles.

> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/change-a-pivotchart-s-layout?u=76281980&t=198)** So for sales, which I have on the vertical, or value axis, I could go up and click Add Chart Element, Axis Titles, Primary Vertical, and then I'll click in the box to edit the title, sales, because the field name of order total doesn't necessarily indicate that we're looking at sales values.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/change-a-pivotchart-s-layout?u=76281980&t=225)** It could be quantity of items ordered.

> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/change-a-pivotchart-s-layout?u=76281980&t=227)** So it makes sense to have sales as an axis title for the vertical axis.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/change-a-pivotchart-s-layout?u=76281980&t=233)** So those are just some of the ways that you can work with the layout on a pivot chart.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/change-a-pivotchart-s-layout?u=76281980&t=237)** You should take the time to experiment with the built-in layouts, and then make any changes that you think best serve your own purposes.

#### Record and review an Excel macro

>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/record-and-review-an-excel-macro?u=76281980&t=86)** And this is a very important step.

>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/record-and-review-an-excel-macro?u=76281980&t=99)** So I'll go up to the Analyze contextual tab, click Clear, and then click Clear All.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/record-and-review-an-excel-macro?u=76281980&t=108)** So I just cleared the pivot table and now I can recreate it.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/record-and-review-an-excel-macro?u=76281980&t=113)** So I'll drag SalesReg to Rows, and then I have OrderType going to Columns.
>

>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/create-relationships-between-tables?u=76281980&t=12)** In this movie, I will show you how to take data that is stored across multiple tables and combine them into a single data model.

#### Edit table relationships
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/edit-table-relationships?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/edit-table-relationships?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] You can define relationships between pairs of Excel tables in your workbook based on common fields, but you might accidentally identify the wrong field in one of the tables.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/edit-table-relationships?u=76281980&t=10)** If that occurs, your pivot table will return incorrect or perhaps no results.

#### Deactivate and delete table relationships
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/deactivate-and-delete-table-relationships?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/deactivate-and-delete-table-relationships?u=76281980&t=10)** In this movie, I will show you how to delete a relationship you no longer need, but also warn you what will happen if you delete a relationship that you do need.

>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/excel-pivottables-in-depth-23781270/deactivate-and-delete-table-relationships?u=76281980&t=167)** Excel can't always do that for you, but it's often worth the trouble to click auto detect to see if there are any relationships that you should have created.
